{"uri": "8187392744", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-20", "time": "16:33:32", "dateTime": "2024-06-20T16:33:32Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-20T16:32:01Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9686274528503418, "url": "https://www.pbs.org/newshour/nation/climate-change-made-heat-wave-in-u-s-mexico-warmer-and-more-likely", "title": "Climate change made heat wave in U.S., Mexico, warmer and more likely", "body": "WASHINGTON (AP) -- Human-caused climate change dialed up the thermostat and turbocharged the odds of this month's killer heat that has been baking the Southwestern United States, Mexico and Central America, a new flash study found.\n\nREAD MORE: How to stay cool and safe during a heat wave\n\nSizzling daytime temperatures that triggered cases of heat stroke in parts of the United States were 35 times more likely and 2.5 degrees hotter (1.4 degrees Celsius) because of the warming from the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, World Weather Attribution, a collection of scientists that run rapid and non-peer reviewed climate attribution studies, calculated Thursday.\n\n\"It's an oven here; you can't stay here,\" 82-year-old Magarita Salazar P\u00e9rez of Veracruz, Mexico, said in her home with no air conditioning. Last week, the Sonoran Desert hit 125 degrees (51.9 degrees Celsius), the hottest day in Mexican history, according to study co-author Shel Winkley, a meteorologist at Climate Central.\n\nAnd it was even worse at night, which is what made this heat wave so deadly, said Imperial College of London climate scientist Friederike Otto, who coordinates the attribution study team. Climate change made nighttime temperatures 2.9 degrees (1.6 degrees Celsius) warmer and unusual evening heat 200 more times more likely, she said.\n\nThere's just been no cool air at night like people are used to, Salazar P\u00e9rez said. Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heat wave.\n\nAt least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\nWATCH: Millions face record-breaking temperatures amid dangerous heat wave\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto told The Associated Press in an interview.\n\n\"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said. Her study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen now than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree (0.5 degrees Celsius) cooler than now. \"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\"\n\nWhile other groups of international scientists -- and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement -- refer to warming since pre-industrial time in mid 1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline - what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study. She said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\"\n\nThe study looked at a large swath of the continent, including southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and the hottest five consecutive days and hottest five consecutive nights. For most of the area, those five days ran from June 3 to 7 and those five nights were June 5 to 9, but in a few places the peak heat started May 26, Otto said.\n\nREAD MORE: After catastrophic floods, Vermont becomes first state to enact law requiring oil companies to pay for damage from climate change\n\nFor example, San Angelo, Texas, hit a record 111 degrees (43.8 degrees Celsius) on June 4. Between June 2 and June 6 the night temperature never dipped below 80 degrees (26.7 degrees Celsius) at Corpus Christi airport, a record each night, with two days when the thermometer never dropped below 85 (29.4 degrees Celsius) according to the National Weather Service.\n\nBetween June 1 and June 15, more than 1,200 daytime high temperature records were tied or broken in the United States and nearly 1,800 nighttime high temperature records were reached, according to the National Center for Environmental Information.\n\nThe attribution team used both current and past temperature measurements, contrasting what is happening to what occurred in past heat waves. They then used the scientifically accepted technique of comparing simulations of a fictional world without human-caused climate change to current reality to come up with how much global warming factored into the 2024 heat wave.\n\nThe immediate meteorological cause was a high pressure system parked over central Mexico that blocked cooling storms and clouds, then it moved to the U.S. Southwest and is now bringing the heat to the U.S. East, Winkley said. Tropical Storm Alberto formed Wednesday and is heading to northern Mexico and southern Texas with some rains, which may cause flooding.\n\nMexico and other places have been dealing for months with drought, water shortages and brutal heat. Monkeys have been dropping from trees in Mexico from the warmth.\n\nThis heat wave \"exacerbates existing inequalities\" between rich and poor in the Americas, Izquierdo said, and Kenkel agreed. The night heat is where the inequalities really become apparent because the ability to cool down with central air conditioning depends on how financially comfortable they are, Kenkel said.\n\nAnd that means during this heat wave Salazar P\u00e9rez has been quite uncomfortable.\n\nF\u00e9lix M\u00e1rquez in Veracruz, Mexico and Teresa de Miguel in Mexico City contributed to this report.", "source": {"uri": "8187619346", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-20", "time": "19:39:13", "dateTime": "2024-06-20T19:39:13Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-20T19:38:15Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9647058844566345, "url": "https://roanoke.com/news/nation-world/weather/climate-change-made-killer-heat-wave-in-mexico-southwest-us-even-warmer-and-35-times/article_893b7118-ec1b-5338-9676-3bb99c89cdf3.html", "title": "Climate change made killer heat wave in Mexico, Southwest US even warmer and 35 times more likely", "body": "WASHINGTON -- Human-caused climate change dialed up the thermostat and turbocharged the odds of this month's killer heat that has been baking the Southwestern United States, Mexico and Central America, a new flash study found.\n\nSizzling daytime temperatures that triggered cases of heat stroke in parts of the United States were 35 times more likely and 2.5 degrees hotter because of the warming from the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, World Weather Attribution, a collection of scientists that run rapid and non-peer reviewed climate attribution studies, calculated Thursday.\n\n\"It's an oven here; you can't stay here,\" 82-year-old Magarita Salazar P\u00e9rez of Veracruz, Mexico, said in her home with no air conditioning. Last week, the Sonoran Desert hit 125 degrees, the hottest day in Mexican history, according to study co-author Shel Winkley, a meteorologist at Climate Central.\n\nIt was even worse at night, which is what made this heat wave so deadly, said Imperial College of London climate scientist Friederike Otto, who coordinates the attribution study team.\n\nClimate change made nighttime temperatures 2.9 degrees warmer and unusual evening heat 200 more times more likely, she said.\n\nThere's just been no cool air at night like people are used to, Salazar P\u00e9rez said. Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heat wave.\n\nAt least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto told The Associated Press.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said. Her study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen now than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree cooler than now. \"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\"\n\nWhile other groups of international scientists -- and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement -- refer to warming since pre-industrial time in mid 1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline -- what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study. She said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\"\n\nThe study looked at a large swath of the continent, including southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and the hottest five consecutive days and hottest five consecutive nights.\n\nFor most of the area, those five days ran from June 3 to 7 and those five nights were June 5 to 9, but in a few places the peak heat started May 26, Otto said.\n\nFor example, San Angelo, Texas, hit a record 111 degrees on June 4. Between June 2 and June 6 the night temperature never dipped below 80 degrees at Corpus Christi airport, a record each night, with two days when the thermometer never dropped below 85 according to the National Weather Service.\n\nBetween June 1 and June 15, more than 1,200 daytime high temperature records were tied or broken in the United States and nearly 1,800 nighttime high temperature records were reached, according to the National Center for Environmental Information.\n\nThe attribution team used both current and past temperature measurements, contrasting what is happening to what occurred in past heat waves. They then used the scientifically accepted technique of comparing simulations of a fictional world without human-caused climate change to current reality to come up with how much global warming factored into the 2024 heat wave.\n\nThe immediate meteorological cause was a high pressure system parked over central Mexico that blocked cooling storms and clouds, then moved to the U.S. Southwest and is now bringing the heat to the U.S. East, Winkley said. Tropical Storm Alberto formed Wednesday and is heading to northern Mexico and southern Texas with some rains, which could cause flooding.\n\nMexico and other places have been dealing for months with drought, water shortages and brutal heat. Monkeys have been dropping from trees in Mexico from the warmth.\n\nThis heat wave \"exacerbates existing inequalities\" between rich and poor in the Americas, Izquierdo said, and Kenkel agreed.\n\nLocal Weather\n\nGet the daily forecast and severe weather alerts in your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or use of this site constitutes agreement to its Last week, the Sonoran Desert hit 125 degrees (51.9 degrees Celsius), the hottest day in Mexican history, according to study co-author Shel Winkley, a meteorologist at Climate Central.\n\nRelated Story: Record-Breaking US Heat Wave Scorches the Midwest as New York Activates the ...\n\nAnd it was even worse at night, which is what made this heat wave so deadly, said Imperial College of London climate scientist Friederike Otto, who coordinates the attribution study team. Climate change made nighttime temperatures 2.9 degrees (1.6 degrees Celsius) warmer and unusual evening heat 200 more times more likely, she said.\n\nThere's just been no cool air at night like people are used to, Salazar P\u00e9rez said. Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heat wave.\n\nAt least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto told The Associated Press in an interview.\n\n\"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said. Her study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen now than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree (0.5 degrees Celsius) cooler than now. \"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\"\n\nRelated Story: California Firefighters Gain Ground Against Big Wildfires After Hot, Windy ...\n\nWhile other groups of international scientists -- and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement -- refer to warming since pre-industrial time in mid 1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline - what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study. She said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\"\n\nThe study looked at a large swath of the continent, including southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and the hottest five consecutive days and hottest five consecutive nights. For most of the area, those five days ran from June 3 to 7 and those five nights were June 5 to 9, but in a few places the peak heat started May 26, Otto said.\n\nFor example, San Angelo, Texas, hit a record 111 degrees (43.8 degrees Celsius) on June 4. Between June 2 and June 6 the night temperature never dipped below 80 degrees (26.7 degrees Celsius) at Corpus Christi airport, a record each night, with two days when the thermometer never dropped below 85 (29.4 degrees Celsius) according to the National Weather Service.\n\nBetween June 1 and June 15, more than 1,200 daytime high temperature records were tied or broken in the United States and nearly 1,800 nighttime high temperature records were reached, according to the National Center for Environmental Information.\n\nRelated Story: Brace Yourselves. Triple-Digit Heat Is Heading Back Here.\n\nThe attribution team used both current and past temperature measurements, contrasting what is happening to what occurred in past heat waves. They then used the scientifically accepted technique of comparing simulations of a fictional world without human-caused climate change to current reality to come up with how much global warming factored into the 2024 heat wave.\n\nThe immediate meteorological cause was a high pressure system parked over central Mexico that blocked cooling storms and clouds, then it moved to the U.S. Southwest and is now bringing the heat to the U.S. East, Winkley said. Tropical Storm Alberto formed Wednesday and is heading to northern Mexico and southern Texas with some rains, which may cause flooding.\n\nMexico and other places have been dealing for months with drought, water shortages and brutal heat. Monkeys have been dropping from trees in Mexico from the warmth.\n\nThis heat wave \"exacerbates existing inequalities\" between rich and poor in the Americas, Izquierdo said, and Kenkel agreed. The night heat is where the inequalities really become apparent because the ability to cool down with central air conditioning depends on how financially comfortable they are, Kenkel said.\n\nAnd that Last week, the Sonoran Desert hit 125 degrees, the hottest day in Mexican history, according to study co-author Shel Winkley, a meteorologist at Climate Central.\n\nIt was even worse at night, which is what made this heat wave so deadly, said Imperial College of London climate scientist Friederike Otto, who coordinates the attribution study team.\n\nClimate change made nighttime temperatures 2.9 degrees warmer and unusual evening heat 200 more times more likely, she said.\n\nThere's just been no cool air at night like people are used to, Salazar P\u00e9rez said. Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heat wave.\n\nAt least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto told The Associated Press.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said. Her study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen now than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree cooler than now. \"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\"\n\nWhile other groups of international scientists -- and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement -- refer to warming since pre-industrial time in mid 1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline -- what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study. She said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\"\n\nThe study looked at a large swath of the continent, including southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and the hottest five consecutive days and hottest five consecutive nights.\n\nFor most of the area, those five days ran from June 3 to 7 and those five nights were June 5 to 9, but in a few places the peak heat started May 26, Otto said.\n\nFor example, San Angelo, Texas, hit a record 111 degrees on June 4. Between June 2 and June 6 the night temperature never dipped below 80 degrees at Corpus Christi airport, a record each night, with two days when the thermometer never dropped below 85 according to the National Weather Service.\n\nBetween June 1 and June 15, more than 1,200 daytime high temperature records were tied or broken in the United States and nearly 1,800 nighttime high temperature records were reached, according to the National Center for Environmental Information.\n\nThe attribution team used both current and past temperature measurements, contrasting what is happening to what occurred in past heat waves. They then used the scientifically accepted technique of comparing simulations of a fictional world without human-caused climate change to current reality to come up with how much global warming factored into the 2024 heat wave.\n\nThe immediate meteorological cause was a high pressure system parked over central Mexico that blocked cooling storms and clouds, then moved to the U.S. Southwest and is now bringing the heat to the U.S. East, Winkley said. Tropical Storm Alberto formed Wednesday and is heading to northern Mexico and southern Texas with some rains, which could cause flooding.\n\nMexico and other places have been dealing for months with drought, water shortages and brutal heat. Monkeys have been dropping from trees in Mexico from the warmth.\n\nThis heat wave \"exacerbates existing inequalities\" between rich and poor in the Americas, Izquierdo said, and Kenkel agreed.\n\nExtreme heat drives up food prices. {"uri": "8187541620", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-20", "time": "18:29:31", "dateTime": "2024-06-20T18:29:31Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-20T18:27:46Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9411764740943909, "url": "https://dothaneagle.com/news/nation-world/weather/climate-change-made-killer-heat-wave-in-mexico-southwest-us-even-warmer-and-35-times/article_f44825a8-a448-56a3-95c4-428a82a1de50.html", "title": "Climate change made killer heat wave in Mexico, Southwest US even warmer and 35 times more likely", "body": "WASHINGTON -- Human-caused climate change dialed up the thermostat and turbocharged the odds of this month's killer heat that has been baking the Southwestern United States, Mexico and Central America, a new flash study found.\n\nSizzling daytime temperatures that triggered cases of heat stroke in parts of the United States were 35 times more likely and 2.5 degrees hotter because of the warming from the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, World Weather Attribution, a collection of scientists that run rapid and non-peer reviewed climate attribution studies, calculated Thursday.\n\n\"It's an oven here; you can't stay here,\" 82-year-old Magarita Salazar P\u00e9rez of Veracruz, Mexico, said in her home with no air conditioning. Last week, the Sonoran Desert hit 125 degrees, the hottest day in Mexican history, according to study co-author Shel Winkley, a meteorologist at Climate Central.\n\nIt was even worse at night, which is what made this heat wave so deadly, said Imperial College of London climate scientist Friederike Otto, who coordinates the attribution study team.\n\nClimate change made nighttime temperatures 2.9 degrees warmer and unusual evening heat 200 more times more likely, she said.\n\nThere's just been no cool air at night like people are used to, Salazar P\u00e9rez said. Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heat wave.\n\nAt least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto told The Associated Press.\n\n\"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said. Her study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen now than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree cooler than now. \"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\"\n\nWhile other groups of international scientists -- and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement -- refer to warming since pre-industrial time in mid 1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline -- what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study. She said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\"\n\nThe study looked at a large swath of the continent, including southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and the hottest five consecutive days and hottest five consecutive nights.\n\nFor most of the area, those five days ran from June 3 to 7 and those five nights were June 5 to 9, but in a few places the peak heat started May 26, Otto said.\n\nFor example, San Angelo, Texas, hit a record 111 degrees on June 4. Between June 2 and June 6 the night temperature never dipped below 80 degrees at Corpus Christi airport, a record each night, with two days when the thermometer never dropped below 85 according to the National Weather Service.\n\nBetween June 1 and June 15, more than 1,200 daytime high temperature records were tied or broken in the United States and nearly 1,800 nighttime high temperature records were reached, according to the National Center for Environmental Information.\n\nThe attribution team used both current and past temperature measurements, contrasting what is happening to what occurred in past heat waves. They then used the scientifically accepted technique of comparing simulations of a fictional world without human-caused climate change to current reality to come up with how much global warming factored into the 2024 heat wave.\n\nThe immediate meteorological cause was a high pressure system parked over central Mexico that blocked cooling storms and clouds, then moved to the U.S. Southwest and is now bringing the heat to the U.S. East, Winkley said. Tropical Storm Alberto formed Wednesday and is heading to northern Mexico and southern Texas with some rains, which could cause flooding.\n\nMexico and other places have been dealing for months with drought, water shortages and brutal heat. Monkeys have been dropping from trees in Mexico from the warmth.\n\nThis heat wave \"exacerbates existing inequalities\" between rich and poor in the Americas, Izquierdo said, and Kenkel agreed.\n\nExtreme heat drives up food prices. {"uri": "2024-06-395543166", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-20", "time": "07:33:33", "dateTime": "2024-06-20T07:33:33Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-20T07:03:17Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9411764740943909, "url": "https://www.seattletimes.com/nation-world/nation/climate-change-made-killer-heat-wave-in-mexico-southwest-us-even-warmer-and-35-times-more-likely/", "title": "Climate change made killer heat wave in Mexico, Southwest US even warmer and 35 times more likely", "body": "WASHINGTON (AP) -- Human-caused climate change dialed up the thermostat and turbocharged the odds of this month's killer heat that has been baking the Southwestern United States, Mexico and Central America, a new flash study found.\n\nSizzling daytime temperatures that triggered cases of heat stroke in parts of the United States were 35 times more likely and 2.5 degrees hotter (1.4 degrees Celsius) because of the warming from the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, World Weather Attribution, a collection of scientists that run rapid and non-peer reviewed climate attribution studies, calculated Thursday.\n\n\"It's an oven here; you can't stay here,\" 82-year-old Magarita Salazar P\u00e9rez of Veracruz, Mexico, said in her home with no air conditioning. Last week, the Sonoran Desert hit 125 degrees (51.9 degrees Celsius), the hottest day in Mexican history, according to study co-author Shel Winkley, a meteorologist at Climate Central.\n\nAnd it was even worse at night, which is what made this heat wave so deadly, said Imperial College of London climate scientist Friederike Otto, who coordinates the attribution study team. Climate change made nighttime temperatures 2.9 degrees (1.6 degrees Celsius) warmer and unusual evening heat 200 more times more likely, she said.\n\nThere's just been no cool air at night like people are used to, Salazar P\u00e9rez said. Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heat wave.\n\nAt least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto told The Associated Press in an interview.\n\n\"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said. Her study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen now than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree (0.5 degrees Celsius) cooler than now. \"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\"\n\nWhile other groups of international scientists -- and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement -- refer to warming since pre-industrial time in mid 1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline - what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study. She said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\"\n\nThe study looked at a large swath of the continent, including southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and the hottest five consecutive days and hottest five consecutive nights. For most of the area, those five days ran from June 3 to 7 and those five nights were June 5 to 9, but in a few places the peak heat started May 26, Otto said.\n\nFor example, San Angelo, Texas, hit a record 111 degrees (43.8 degrees Celsius) on June 4. Between June 2 and June 6 the night temperature never dipped below 80 degrees (26.7 degrees Celsius) at Corpus Christi airport, a record each night, with two days when the thermometer never dropped below 85 (29.4 degrees Celsius) according to the National Weather Service.\n\nBetween June 1 and June 15, more than 1,200 daytime high temperature records were tied or broken in the United States and nearly 1,800 nighttime high temperature records were reached, according to the National Center for Environmental Information.\n\nThe attribution team used both current and past temperature measurements, contrasting what is happening to what occurred in past heat waves. They then used the scientifically accepted technique of comparing simulations of a fictional world without human-caused climate change to current reality to come up with how much global warming factored into the 2024 heat wave.\n\nThe immediate meteorological cause was a high pressure system parked over central Mexico that blocked cooling storms and clouds, then it moved to the U.S. Southwest and is now bringing the heat to the U.S. East, Winkley said. Tropical Storm Alberto formed Wednesday and is heading to northern Mexico and southern Texas with some rains, which may cause flooding.\n\nMexico and other places have been dealing for months with drought, water shortages and brutal heat. Monkeys have been dropping from trees in Mexico from the warmth.\n\nThis heat wave \"exacerbates existing inequalities\" between rich and poor in the Americas, Izquierdo said, and Kenkel agreed. The night heat is where the inequalities really become apparent because the ability to cool down with central air conditioning depends on how financially comfortable they are, Kenkel said.\n\nAnd that means during this heat wave Salazar P\u00e9rez has been quite uncomfortable.\n\n___\n\nF\u00e9lix M\u00e1rquez in Veracruz, Mexico and Teresa de Miguel in Mexico City contributed to this report.\n\n___\n\nRead more of AP's climate coverage at http://www.apnews.com/climate-and-environment\n\nThe Associated Press' climate and environmental coverage receives financial support from multiple private foundations. {"uri": "8186519767", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-20", "time": "07:20:34", "dateTime": "2024-06-20T07:20:34Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-20T07:19:30Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9372549057006836, "url": "https://www.newsday.com/news/nation/heat-wave-climate-change-causes-blame-d47919", "title": "Climate change made killer heat wave in Mexico, Southwest US even warmer and 35 times more likely", "body": "WASHINGTON -- Human-caused climate change dialed up the thermostat and turbocharged the odds of this month's killer heat that has been baking the Southwestern United States, Mexico and Central America, a new flash study found.\n\nSizzling daytime temperatures that triggered cases of heat stroke in parts of the United States were 35 times more likely and 2.5 degrees hotter (1.4 degrees Celsius) because of the warming from the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, World Weather Attribution, a collection of scientists that run rapid and non-peer reviewed climate attribution studies, calculated Thursday.\n\n\"It's an oven here; you can't stay here,\" 82-year-old Magarita Salazar P\u00e9rez of Veracruz, Mexico, said in her home with no air conditioning. Last week, the Sonoran Desert hit 125 degrees (51.9 degrees Celsius), the hottest day in Mexican history, according to study co-author Shel Winkley, a meteorologist at Climate Central.\n\nAnd it was even worse at night, which is what made this heat wave so deadly, said Imperial College of London climate scientist Friederike Otto, who coordinates the attribution study team. Climate change made nighttime temperatures 2.9 degrees (1.6 degrees Celsius) warmer and unusual evening heat 200 more times more likely, she said.\n\nThere's just been no cool air at night like people are used to, Salazar P\u00e9rez said. Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heat wave.\n\nAt least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto told The Associated Press in an interview.\n\n\"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said. Her study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen now than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree (0.5 degrees Celsius) cooler than now. \"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\"\n\nWhile other groups of international scientists -- and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement -- refer to warming since pre-industrial time in mid 1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline - what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study. She said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\"\n\nThe study looked at a large swath of the continent, including southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and the hottest five consecutive days and hottest five consecutive nights. For most of the area, those five days ran from June 3 to 7 and those five nights were June 5 to 9, but in a few places the peak heat started May 26, Otto said.\n\nFor example, San Angelo, Texas, hit a record 111 degrees (43.8 degrees Celsius) on June 4. Between June 2 and June 6 the night temperature never dipped below 80 degrees (26.7 degrees Celsius) at Corpus Christi airport, a record each night, with two days when the thermometer never dropped below 85 (29.4 degrees Celsius) according to the National Weather Service.\n\nBetween June 1 and June 15, more than 1,200 daytime high temperature records were tied or broken in the United States and nearly 1,800 nighttime high temperature records were reached, according to the National Center for Environmental Information.\n\nThe attribution team used both current and past temperature measurements, contrasting what is happening to what occurred in past heat waves. They then used the scientifically accepted technique of comparing simulations of a fictional world without human-caused climate change to current reality to come up with how much global warming factored into the 2024 heat wave.\n\nThe immediate meteorological cause was a high pressure system parked over central Mexico that blocked cooling storms and clouds, then it moved to the U.S. Southwest and is now bringing the heat to the U.S. East, Winkley said. Tropical Storm Alberto formed Wednesday and is heading to northern Mexico and southern Texas with some rains, which may cause flooding.\n\nMexico and other places have been dealing for months with drought, water shortages and brutal heat. Monkeys have been dropping from trees in Mexico from the warmth.\n\nThis heat wave \"exacerbates existing inequalities\" between rich and poor in the Americas, Izquierdo said, and Kenkel agreed. The night heat is where the inequalities really become apparent because the ability to cool down with central air conditioning depends on how financially comfortable they are, Kenkel said.\n\nAnd that means during this heat wave Salazar P\u00e9rez has been quite uncomfortable.\n\n___\n\nF\u00e9lix M\u00e1rquez in Veracruz, Mexico and Teresa de Miguel in Mexico City contributed to this report.\n\n___\n\nRead more of AP's climate coverage at http://www.apnews.com/climate-and-environment", "source": {"uri": "newsday.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "Newsday"}, "authors": [], {"uri": "2024-06-395977217", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-20", "time": "13:36:02", "dateTime": "2024-06-20T13:36:02Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-20T13:35:47Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.929411768913269, "url": "http://english.ahram.org.eg/NewsContent/2/9/525752/World/International/Climate-change-made-killer-heatwave-in-Mexico,-Sou.aspx", "title": "Climate change made killer heatwave in Mexico, Southwest US", "body": "Sizzling daytime temperatures that triggered cases of heat stroke in parts of the United States were 35 times more likely and 2.5 degrees hotter (1.4 degrees Celsius) because of the warming from the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, World Weather Attribution, a collection of scientists that run rapid and non-peer reviewed climate attribution studies, calculated Thursday.\n\n\"It's an oven here; you can't stay here,\" 82-year-old Margarita Salazar P\u00e9rez of Veracruz, Mexico, said in her home with no air conditioning. Last week, the Sonoran Desert hit 125 degrees (51.9 degrees Celsius), the hottest day in Mexican history, according to study co-author Shel Winkley, a meteorologist at Climate Central.\n\nAnd it was even worse at night, which is what made this heat wave so deadly, said Imperial College of London climate scientist Friederike Otto, who coordinates the attribution study team. Climate change made nighttime temperatures 2.9 degrees (1.6 degrees Celsius) warmer and unusual evening heat 200 times more likely, she said.\n\nThere's just been no cool air at night like people are used to, Salazar P\u00e9rez said. Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heat wave.\n\nAt least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense, it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto told The Associated Press in an interview.\n\n\"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said. Her study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree (0.5 degrees Celsius) cooler than now. \"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\"\n\nWhile other groups of international scientists -- and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement -- refer to warming since pre-industrial time in the mid-1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline - what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study. She said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\"\n\nThe study looked at a large swath of the continent, including southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and the hottest five consecutive days and hottest five consecutive nights. For most of the area, those five days ran from June 3 to 7 and those five nights were June 5 to 9, but in a few places the peak heat started May 26, Otto said.\n\nFor example, San Angelo, Texas, hit a record 111 degrees (43.8 degrees Celsius) on June 4. Between June 2 and June 6, the night temperature never dipped below 80 degrees (26.7 degrees Celsius) at Corpus Christi airport, a record each night, with two days when the thermometer never dropped below 85 (29.4 degrees Celsius) according to the National Weather Service.\n\nBetween June 1 and June 15, more than 1,200 daytime high temperature records were tied or broken in the United States and nearly 1,800 nighttime high temperature records were reached, according to the National Center for Environmental Information.\n\nThe attribution team used both current and past temperature measurements, contrasting what is happening to what occurred in past heat waves. They then used the scientifically accepted technique of comparing simulations of a fictional world without human-caused climate change to current reality to come up with how much global warming factored into the 2024 heat wave.\n\nThe immediate meteorological cause was a high-pressure system parked over central Mexico that blocked cooling storms and clouds, then it moved to the U.S. Southwest and is now bringing the heat to the U.S. East, Winkley said. Tropical Storm Alberto formed Wednesday and is heading to northern Mexico and southern Texas with some rains, which may cause flooding.\n\nMexico and other places have been dealing for months with drought, water shortages and brutal heat. Monkeys have been dropping from trees in Mexico from the warmth.\n\nThis heat wave \"exacerbates existing inequalities\" between rich and poor in the Americas, Izquierdo said, and Kenkel agreed. The night heat is where the inequalities become apparent because the ability to cool down with central air conditioning depends on how financially comfortable they are, Kenkel said.\n\nAnd that means Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heat wave.\n\nAt least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto told The Associated Press in an interview.\n\n\"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said. Her study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen now than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree cooler than now. \"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\"\n\nWhile other groups of international scientists - and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement - refer to warming since pre-industrial time in mid 1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline - what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study. She said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\"\n\nThe study looked at a large swath of the continent, including Southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and the hottest five consecutive days and hottest five consecutive nights. For most of the area, those five days ran from June 3 to 7 and those five nights were June 5 to 9, but in a few places the peak heat started May 26, Otto said.\n\nFor example, San Angelo, Texas, hit a record 111 degrees on June 4. Between June 2 and June 6, the night temperature never dipped below 80 degrees at Corpus Christi airport, a record each night, with two days when the thermometer never dropped below 85, according to the National Weather Service.\n\nBetween June 1 and June 15, more than 1,200 daytime high temperature records were tied or broken in the United States and nearly 1,800 nighttime high temperature records were reached, according to the National Center for Environmental Information.\n\nThe attribution team used both current and past temperature measurements, contrasting what's happening to what occurred in past heat waves. They then used the scientifically accepted technique of comparing simulations of a fictional world without human-caused climate change to current reality to come up with how much global warming factored into the 2024 heat wave.\n\nThe immediate meteorological cause was a high pressure system parked over central Mexico that blocked cooling storms and clouds, then moved to the U.S. Southwest and is now bringing the heat to the U.S. East, Winkley said. Tropical Storm Alberto formed Wednesday and headed to northern Mexico and southern Texas with some rains, which may cause flooding.\n\nMexico and other places have been dealing for months with drought, water shortages and brutal heat. Monkeys have been dropping from trees in Mexico from the warmth.\n\nThis heat wave \"exacerbates existing inequalities\" between rich and poor in the Americas, Izquierdo said, and Kenkel agreed. The night heat is where the inequalities really become apparent because the ability to cool down with central air conditioning depends on how financially comfortable they are, Kenkel said.\n\nAnd that Last week, the Sonoran Desert hit 125 degrees (51.9 degrees Celsius), the hottest day in Mexican history, according to study co-author Shel Winkley, a meteorologist at Climate Central.\n\nAnd it was even worse at night, which is what made this heat wave so deadly, said Imperial College of London climate scientist Friederike Otto, who coordinates the attribution study team. Climate change made nighttime temperatures 2.9 degrees (1.6 degrees Celsius) warmer and unusual evening heat 200 more times more likely, she said.\n\nThere's just been no cool air at night like people are used to, Salazar P\u00e9rez said. Last week, the Sonoran Desert hit 125 degrees (51.9 degrees Celsius), the hottest day in Mexican history, according to study co-author Shel Winkley, a meteorologist at Climate Central.\n\nAnd it was even worse at night, which is what made this heat wave so deadly, said Imperial College of London climate scientist Friederike Otto, who coordinates the attribution study team. Climate change made nighttime temperatures 2.9 degrees (1.6 degrees Celsius) warmer and unusual evening heat 200 more times more likely, she said.\n\nThere's just been no cool air at night like people are used to, Salazar P\u00e9rez said. Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heatwave. At least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto said. \"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said.\n\nHer study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen now than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree (0.5 degrees Celsius) cooler than now.\n\n\"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\" While other groups of international scientists -- and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement -- refer to warming since pre-industrial time in mid 1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline - what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study.\n\nShe said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\" The study looked at a large swath of the continent, including southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heat wave.\n\nAt least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto told The Associated Press.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said. Her study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen now than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree cooler than now. \"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\"\n\nWhile other groups of international scientists -- and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement -- refer to warming since pre-industrial time in mid 1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline -- what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study. She said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\"\n\nThe study looked at a large swath of the continent, including southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and the hottest five consecutive days and hottest five consecutive nights.\n\nFor most of the area, those five days ran from June 3 to 7 and those five nights were June 5 to 9, but in a few places the peak heat started May 26, Otto said.\n\nFor example, San Angelo, Texas, hit a record 111 degrees on June 4. Between June 2 and June 6 the night temperature never dipped below 80 degrees at Corpus Christi airport, a record each night, with two days when the thermometer never dropped below 85 according to the National Weather Service.\n\nBetween June 1 and June 15, more than 1,200 daytime high temperature records were tied or broken in the United States and nearly 1,800 nighttime high temperature records were reached, according to the National Center for Environmental Information.\n\nThe attribution team used both current and past temperature measurements, contrasting what is happening to what occurred in past heat waves. They then used the scientifically accepted technique of comparing simulations of a fictional world without human-caused climate change to current reality to come up with how much global warming factored into the 2024 heat wave.\n\nThe immediate meteorological cause was a high pressure system parked over central Mexico that blocked cooling storms and clouds, then moved to the U.S. Southwest and is now bringing the heat to the U.S. East, Winkley said. Tropical Storm Alberto formed Wednesday and is heading to northern Mexico and southern Texas with some rains, which could cause flooding.\n\nMexico and other places have been dealing for months with drought, water shortages and brutal heat. Monkeys have been dropping from trees in Mexico from the warmth.\n\nThis heat wave \"exacerbates existing inequalities\" between rich and poor in the Americas, Izquierdo said, and Kenkel agreed.\n\nExtreme heat drives up food prices. {"uri": "8187543773", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-20", "time": "18:30:22", "dateTime": "2024-06-20T18:30:22Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-20T18:29:36Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8705882430076599, "url": "https://siouxcityjournal.com/news/nation-world/weather/climate-change-made-killer-heat-wave-in-mexico-southwest-us-even-warmer-and-35-times/article_b74184c5-dbfb-5c08-ad90-f4bb92df8ad2.html", "title": "Climate change made killer heat wave in Mexico, Southwest US even warmer and 35 times more likely", "body": "WASHINGTON -- Human-caused climate change dialed up the thermostat and turbocharged the odds of this month's killer heat that has been baking the Southwestern United States, Mexico and Central America, a new flash study found.\n\nSizzling daytime temperatures that triggered cases of heat stroke in parts of the United States were 35 times more likely and 2.5 degrees hotter because of the warming from the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, World Weather Attribution, a collection of scientists that run rapid and non-peer reviewed climate attribution studies, calculated Thursday.\n\n\"It's an oven here; you can't stay here,\" 82-year-old Magarita Salazar P\u00e9rez of Veracruz, Mexico, said in her home with no air conditioning. Last week, the Sonoran Desert hit 125 degrees, the hottest day in Mexican history, according to study co-author Shel Winkley, a meteorologist at Climate Central.\n\nPeople are also reading... Iowa DCI: Wall Lake trucker David Schultz's death 'not a homicide' Jefferson, S.D. dad shares secret to fatherhood on Father's Day celebration Lost and found: How Arnolds Park got a miniature train back after more than 50 years Perley to step down as Thompson CEO; Beaulieu to take over Man dies in standoff with authorities in Sac County Former Sioux City hotel under renovation, to become high-rise senior housing this year Omaha ballpark seeks to ban two Texas A&M fans who were kicked out of College World Series game Tyson Foods heir suspended as CFO after second alcohol-related arrest Boat speed rule imposed on Okoboji lakes in wake of heavy rains, storms that also led to wastewater discharges Sioux City man pleads not guilty of manslaughter Our Opinion: WITCC settlement in human trafficking case raises more questions Marriage licenses for Woodbury County for June 17, 2024 Chainsaw sculptures among latest upgrades to Sioux City's historic Hubbard Park Top area softball performers: RSM last unbeaten team; Mentzer, Moreau swinging hot bats Man pleads not guilty in Sioux City fatal hit and run\n\nIt was even worse at night, which is what made this heat wave so deadly, said Imperial College of London climate scientist Friederike Otto, who coordinates the attribution study team.\n\nClimate change made nighttime temperatures 2.9 degrees warmer and unusual evening heat 200 more times more likely, she said.\n\nThere's just been no cool air at night like people are used to, Salazar P\u00e9rez said. Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heat wave.\n\nAt least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto told The Associated Press.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said. Her study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen now than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree cooler than now. \"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\"\n\nWhile other groups of international scientists -- and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement -- refer to warming since pre-industrial time in mid 1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline -- what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study. She said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\"\n\nThe study looked at a large swath of the continent, including southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and the hottest five consecutive days and hottest five consecutive nights.\n\nFor most of the area, those five days ran from June 3 to 7 and those five nights were June 5 to 9, but in a few places the peak heat started May 26, Otto said.\n\nFor example, San Angelo, Texas, hit a record 111 degrees on June 4. Between June 2 and June 6 the night temperature never dipped below 80 degrees at Corpus Christi airport, a record each night, with two days when the thermometer never dropped below 85 according to the National Weather Service.\n\nBetween June 1 and June 15, more than 1,200 daytime high temperature records were tied or broken in the United States and nearly 1,800 nighttime high temperature records were reached, according to the National Center for Environmental Information.\n\nThe attribution team used both current and past temperature measurements, contrasting what is happening to what occurred in past heat waves. They then used the scientifically accepted technique of comparing simulations of a fictional world without human-caused climate change to current reality to come up with how much global warming factored into the 2024 heat wave.\n\nThe immediate meteorological cause was a high pressure system parked over central Mexico that blocked cooling storms and clouds, then moved to the U.S. Southwest and is now bringing the heat to the U.S. East, Winkley said. Tropical Storm Alberto formed Wednesday and is heading to northern Mexico and southern Texas with some rains, which could cause flooding.\n\nMexico and other places have been dealing for months with drought, water shortages and brutal heat. Monkeys have been dropping from trees in Mexico from the warmth.\n\nThis heat wave \"exacerbates existing inequalities\" between rich and poor in the Americas, Izquierdo said, and Kenkel agreed.\n\nExtreme heat drives up food prices. {"uri": "8187574721", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-20", "time": "18:58:15", "dateTime": "2024-06-20T18:58:15Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-20T18:56:28Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8627451062202454, "url": "https://www.stltoday.com/news/nation-world/weather/climate-change-made-killer-heat-wave-in-mexico-southwest-us-even-warmer-and-35-times/article_f633f222-075e-5ad9-b517-a2786b7e4f68.html", "title": "Climate change made killer heat wave in Mexico, Southwest US even warmer and 35 times more likely", "body": "WASHINGTON -- Human-caused climate change dialed up the thermostat and turbocharged the odds of this month's killer heat that has been baking the Southwestern United States, Mexico and Central America, a new flash study found.\n\nSizzling daytime temperatures that triggered cases of heat stroke in parts of the United States were 35 times more likely and 2.5 degrees hotter because of the warming from the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, World Weather Attribution, a collection of scientists that run rapid and non-peer reviewed climate attribution studies, calculated Thursday.\n\n\"It's an oven here; you can't stay here,\" 82-year-old Magarita Salazar P\u00e9rez of Veracruz, Mexico, said in her home with no air conditioning. Last week, the Sonoran Desert hit 125 degrees, the hottest day in Mexican history, according to study co-author Shel Winkley, a meteorologist at Climate Central.\n\nInvestigator tracked Kim Gardner to health clinic as St. Louis prosecutor's office crashed Here are 13 must-visit restaurants in the St. Louis area from Ian Froeb's newsletter New Channel 32? Established stations 11, 5, BSM? Sports TV intrigue continues: Media Views Cardinals broadcaster Bally Sports 'stuck in the middle' in dispute over contracts Hazelwood woman killed by tree branch in trimming accident UFL officials say they expect to grow after inaugural season, capped by Birmingham's title Police shoot, kill teen they say led them on chase and pointed gun in north St. Louis A sneak peek at St. Louis Zoo's new park, where animals are already busy Sam Page offered job to St. Louis County council candidate, asked her to weigh quitting race St. Louis paying 'guaranteed basic income' to 540 families is under fire in lawsuit St. Louis to pay homeless to clean streets and pick up trash The 'Bad Dad Joke House' brings laughter to St. Louis Cardinals, Andre Pallante left with plenty of what ifs after 5-1 loss to the Cubs How to watch Sunday's Cardinals-Cubs game, which airs exclusively on Roku McClellan: A simple life until a glitch got in the way\n\nIt was even worse at night, which is what made this heat wave so deadly, said Imperial College of London climate scientist Friederike Otto, who coordinates the attribution study team.\n\nClimate change made nighttime temperatures 2.9 degrees warmer and unusual evening heat 200 more times more likely, she said.\n\nThere's just been no cool air at night like people are used to, Salazar P\u00e9rez said. Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heat wave.\n\nAt least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto told The Associated Press.\n\n\"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said. Her study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen now than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree cooler than now. \"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\"\n\nWhile other groups of international scientists -- and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement -- refer to warming since pre-industrial time in mid 1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline -- what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study. She said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\"\n\nThe study looked at a large swath of the continent, including southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and the hottest five consecutive days and hottest five consecutive nights.\n\nFor most of the area, those five days ran from June 3 to 7 and those five nights were June 5 to 9, but in a few places the peak heat started May 26, Otto said.\n\nFor example, San Angelo, Texas, hit a record 111 degrees on June 4. Between June 2 and June 6 the night temperature never dipped below 80 degrees at Corpus Christi airport, a record each night, with two days when the thermometer never dropped below 85 according to the National Weather Service.\n\nBetween June 1 and June 15, more than 1,200 daytime high temperature records were tied or broken in the United States and nearly 1,800 nighttime high temperature records were reached, according to the National Center for Environmental Information.\n\nThe attribution team used both current and past temperature measurements, contrasting what is happening to what occurred in past heat waves. They then used the scientifically accepted technique of comparing simulations of a fictional world without human-caused climate change to current reality to come up with how much global warming factored into the 2024 heat wave.\n\nThe immediate meteorological cause was a high pressure system parked over central Mexico that blocked cooling storms and clouds, then moved to the U.S. Southwest and is now bringing the heat to the U.S. East, Winkley said. Tropical Storm Alberto formed Wednesday and is heading to northern Mexico and southern Texas with some rains, which could cause flooding.\n\nMexico and other places have been dealing for months with drought, water shortages and brutal heat. Monkeys have been dropping from trees in Mexico from the warmth.\n\nThis heat wave \"exacerbates existing inequalities\" between rich and poor in the Americas, Izquierdo said, and Kenkel agreed.\n\n0 {"uri": "2024-06-396297719", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-06-20", "time": "18:44:28", "dateTime": "2024-06-20T18:44:28Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-06-20T18:15:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8470588326454163, "url": "https://journalstar.com/news/nation-world/weather/climate-change-made-killer-heat-wave-in-mexico-southwest-us-even-warmer-and-35-times/article_598c2ddf-338e-59c7-8013-20d6071e1f5e.html", "title": "Climate change made killer heat wave in Mexico, Southwest US even warmer and 35 times more likely", "body": "As climate change renders heatwaves increasingly common and severe, some parts of the world are nearing the threshold where healthy people are at risk of getting ill and even dying in the extreme heat, says Chloe Brimicombe, a climate scientist at the University of Graz's Wegener Centre.\n\nWASHINGTON -- Human-caused climate change dialed up the thermostat and turbocharged the odds of this month's killer heat that has been baking the Southwestern United States, Mexico and Central America, a new flash study found.\n\nSizzling daytime temperatures that triggered cases of heat stroke in parts of the United States were 35 times more likely and 2.5 degrees hotter because of the warming from the burning of coal, oil and natural gas, World Weather Attribution, a collection of scientists that run rapid and non-peer reviewed climate attribution studies, calculated Thursday.\n\n\"It's an oven here; you can't stay here,\" 82-year-old Magarita Salazar P\u00e9rez of Veracruz, Mexico, said in her home with no air conditioning. Last week, the Sonoran Desert hit 125 degrees, the hottest day in Mexican history, according to study co-author Shel Winkley, a meteorologist at Climate Central.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nLincoln ABC affiliate KLKN parts ways with evening anchor Lincoln teen who drowned Thursday in Holmes Lake identified Nebraska volleyball's Harper Murray charged with shoplifting from sporting goods store Omaha ballpark seeks to ban two Texas A&M fans who were kicked out of College World Series game Lincoln's two wind turbines to come down next month Reported birth of rare white buffalo calf in Yellowstone park fulfills Lakota prophecy Jimmy Fallon to Omaha volleyball players during chance meeting in Rome: 'You guys are tall' Recruiting: Nebraska set to host eight official visitors, including offensive lineman from New Zealand 'I ... made sure he didn't die on us:' Lincoln men save friend after hit-and-run 'Knew in my heart': Late-night calls lands Nebraska volleyball its first 2026 commitment Report: 2 Nebraska biofuel plants among biggest emitters of toxic pollutants 40-year-old Lincoln woman arrested for role in fatal crash, police say Omaha's zoo unveils $21 million orangutan forest exhibit: 'It's a really immersive experience' 1 killed, 1 hurt in stabbing at Grand Island-area rest stop; man arrested after chase New features, including slides and a treehouse, added to Nebraska state parks this summer\n\nIt was even worse at night, which is what made this heat wave so deadly, said Imperial College of London climate scientist Friederike Otto, who coordinates the attribution study team.\n\nClimate change made nighttime temperatures 2.9 degrees warmer and unusual evening heat 200 more times more likely, she said.\n\nThere's just been no cool air at night like people are used to, Salazar P\u00e9rez said. Doctors say cooler night temperatures are key to surviving a heat wave.\n\nAt least 125 people have died so far, according to the World Weather Attribution team.\n\n\"This is clearly related to climate change, the level of intensity that we are seeing, these risks,\" said study co-author Karina Izquierdo, a Mexico City-based urban advisor for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Climate Centre.\n\nThe alarming part about this heat wave, which technically is still cooking the North American continent, is that it's no longer that out of the ordinary anymore, Otto said. Past studies by the group have looked at heat so extreme that they found it impossible without climate change, but this heat wave not so much.\n\n\"From a sort of weather perspective in that sense it wasn't rare, but the impacts were actually really bad,\" Otto told The Associated Press.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\n\"The changes we have seen in the last 20 years, which feels like just yesterday, are so strong,\" Otto said. Her study found that this heat wave is now four times more likely to happen now than it was in the year 2000 when it was nearly a degree cooler than now. \"It seems sort of far away and a different world.\"\n\nWhile other groups of international scientists -- and the global carbon emissions reduction target adopted by countries in the 2015 Paris climate agreement -- refer to warming since pre-industrial time in mid 1800s, Otto said comparing what's happening now to the year 2000 is more striking.\n\n\"We're looking at a shifting baseline -- what was once extreme but rare is becoming increasingly common,\" said University of Southern California Marine Studies Chair Carly Kenkel, who wasn't part of the attribution team's study. She said the analysis is \"the logical conclusion based on the data.\"\n\nThe study looked at a large swath of the continent, including southern California, Arizona, New Mexico, Texas, Oklahoma, Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Belize and Honduras and the hottest five consecutive days and hottest five consecutive nights.\n\nFor most of the area, those five days ran from June 3 to 7 and those five nights were June 5 to 9, but in a few places the peak heat started May 26, Otto said.\n\nFor example, San Angelo, Texas, hit a record 111 degrees on June 4. Between June 2 and June 6 the night temperature never dipped below 80 degrees at Corpus Christi airport, a record each night, with two days when the thermometer never dropped below 85 according to the National Weather Service.\n\nBetween June 1 and June 15, more than 1,200 daytime high temperature records were tied or broken in the United States and nearly 1,800 nighttime high temperature records were reached, according to the National Center for Environmental Information.\n\nThe attribution team used both current and past temperature measurements, contrasting what is happening to what occurred in past heat waves. They then used the scientifically accepted technique of comparing simulations of a fictional world without human-caused climate change to current reality to come up with how much global warming factored into the 2024 heat wave.\n\nThe immediate meteorological cause was a high pressure system parked over central Mexico that blocked cooling storms and clouds, then moved to the U.S. Southwest and is now bringing the heat to the U.S. East, Winkley said. Tropical Storm Alberto formed Wednesday and is heading to northern Mexico and southern Texas with some rains, which could cause flooding.\n\nMexico and other places have been dealing for months with drought, water shortages and brutal heat. Monkeys have been dropping from trees in Mexico from the warmth.\n\nThis heat wave \"exacerbates existing inequalities\" between rich and poor in the Americas, Izquierdo said, and Kenkel agreed.\n\nExtreme heat drives up food prices.
